Similarly, horizontal lines: sizes 7,6,5,4,3,2,1. So any three horizontal lines cover at most 18 points. Slope -1 lines: sums c from 2 to 8, sizes 1,2,3,4,5,6,7. So any three such lines cover at most 7+6+5 = 18 points. Thus, any three non-sunny lines can cover at most 18 points (if they are all of the same type, say vertical, the sum of sizes of three columns is at most 7+6+5 = 18; if they are of different types, the union may be smaller). So the maximum number of points covered by three non-sunny lines is 18. That's an important observation! Let's verify: For vertical lines, the sizes are decreasing. The three largest sizes are 7,6,5, sum 18. For horizontal lines, similarly. For diagonal lines, sizes are 1,2,3,4,5,6,7; three largest are 7,6,5 sum 18. So indeed, any three lines of these types can cover at most 18 points from T_n. This is because each such line covers a set of points that is a column, row, or diagonal. The maximum union size of three such lines is at most 18. This seems plausible. However, we need to consider that lines of different types may intersect and cause overlapping, but the union size cannot exceed the sum of the sizes of the individual lines. But we need a bound on the maximum possible union size of three non-sunny lines. Since the sizes of these lines vary, the maximum union size occurs when we take the three lines that have the largest individual sizes and are as disjoint as possible. But can we achieve union size 18? Yes, by taking three vertical lines at x=1,2,3 (sum 18). Similarly, three horizontal lines at y=1,2,3 sum 18. So the maximum is 18. Therefore, if we have m non-sunny lines, they can cover at most m * n points? No, that's not a tight bound because the sizes of lines are not all n; but if we take m lines, the total union size could be larger than 18 if m>3. But we are interested in the case where k ≥ 4 → m ≤ n-4. For n=7, m=3, maximum union is 18. For n=6, m=2, maximum union is at most 7+6=13 (if we take x=1 and x=2). That's 13. So the non-sunny lines cover at most something. The sunny lines cover at most something else. Then the total union must be N. The sunny lines are k in number. Each sunny line can contain at most D = floor((n+1)/2) points (achieved by slope 1). So the maximum points that can be covered by k sunny lines is at most k * D. But that's not a bound on union; they could overlap. However, we can also bound the number of points that can be covered by k sunny lines if we consider that they are not parallel to any of the special directions, and also that they can intersect each other. The maximum union size from k sunny lines could be as large as k * D (if they are disjoint). But they may not be disjoint. So we have an upper bound.

Now, if we have k ≥ 4, then m = n - k ≤ n - 4. The maximum union size from m non-sunny lines is at most the sum of the sizes of the m largest such lines. Since the sizes are decreasing, the sum of the m largest sizes among the columns/rows/diagonals is something like sum_{i=1}^{m} (n+1-i) = m*n - m(m+1)/2? Wait careful: The columns have sizes n, n-1, ..., 1. So the sum of the m largest column sizes is sum_{i=0}^{m-1} (n - i) = m*n - (m-1)m/2? Actually sum_{i=0}^{m-1} (n - i) = m*n - (m-1)m/2. That's for vertical lines. Similarly for horizontal lines. For diagonal lines, sizes are 1,2,...,n. The m largest sizes are n, n-1, ..., n-m+1, sum = m*n - (m-1)m/2 as well. So the maximum union size from m non-sunny lines is at most that sum, if we choose lines of the type that have large sizes and are disjoint (like vertical lines at a=1..m). But can we achieve that sum? Yes, by taking vertical lines at x=1,2,...,m. That covers columns 1..m, which are disjoint, union size = sum_{i=1}^{m} (n+1-i) = m*n - m(m+1)/2? Let's compute: for a=1, size = n; a=2, size = n-1; ... a=m, size = n-m+1. Sum = m*n - (1+2+...+(m-1))? Actually sum_{i=1}^{m} (n - i + 1) = m*n - (0+1+...+(m-1)) = m*n - (m-1)m/2. That's the same as m*n - m(m-1)/2. For m=3, n=7: 21 - 3 = 18. That's correct. So the maximum union size from m non-sunny lines is U_max(m) = m*n - m(m-1)/2. However, is it always possible to choose m non-sunny lines that are all of the same type (vertical) and cover those columns? Yes, because we can choose lines x=1, x=2, ..., x=m. Those are distinct non-sunny lines. So we can achieve that union. So if we have m non-sunny lines, they can cover at most that many points (if we choose them optimally). But we are not forced to choose them optimally; we are trying to cover T_n with some set of n lines. But we need a lower bound on the union size: the union must be at least N. So we need U_max(m) + k*D ≥ N, which holds for n=8 (26+16=42≥36). So that doesn't rule out.

## Solution
We'll produce a comprehensive solution.

First, restate problem.

We need to determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane with exactly k of them being sunny (not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or line x+y=0) and for all positive integers a,b with a+b ≤ n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines.

We'll show that the only possible k are 0,1,3.  
We'll give constructions for k=0,1,3 and prove impossibility for k=2 and k≥4.

We'll also note that the answer is independent of n (except that n≥3).  

But we need to be careful: for n=3, k=3 is possible, but also k=1,0. So the set {0,1,3} works. For n=4,5,... also. So answer is {0,1,3}.

Now, provide constructions.

- k=0: take lines L_c: x+y = c for c=2,3,…,n+1. These are distinct, not sunny (parallel to x+y=0). They cover all points (a,b) with a+b ≤ n+1. Good.

- k=1: we need a set of n lines, exactly one sunny, covering T_n. Construction: Let L_1 be the vertical line x = 1 (non‑sunny). Let L_2 be the horizontal line y = 1 (non‑sunny). For the remaining n-2 lines, take the lines x+y = c for c = 3,4,…, n+1? But we must ensure that all points are covered, and that exactly one line is sunny. However, the lines x+y = c are non‑sunny. So that would give k=0. So we need to include a sunny line. Instead, we can use the construction we found for n=4 and generalize. Let's design a general construction with k=1 for any n≥3.

We can take the following set of n lines:
- L_1: x = 1 (vertical, non‑sunny)
- L_2: y = 1 (horizontal, non‑sunny)
- For i = 3,…,n: take the line L_i: x + y = i + 1? That would be n-2 lines (since from 3 to n gives n-2 lines). But we need n lines total. So we have 2 + (n-2) = n lines. However, all those lines are non‑sunny. So k=0. Not good.

We need exactly 1 sunny line. So we need to replace some of the non‑sunny lines with a sunny line. For example, take the line y = x (sunny). Then we need to cover the points that are not on that line with non‑sunny lines. But we also need to cover the points on y=x. The points on y=x are those with a=b, a from 1 to floor((n+1)/2). That's about n/2 points. If we include y=x, then we need to cover the rest of T_n with n-1 non‑sunny lines. Can we do that? The rest is T_n minus that diagonal. That's a set of points that can be covered by n-1 lines of slope -1 (diagonals) maybe. But we need to ensure that the covering lines are distinct and cover all points. Let's attempt a systematic construction.

We observed that for n=4, the set {y=x, x=1, y=1, x+y=5} works. That's 4 lines, with y=x sunny, the others non‑sunny. So we have a configuration with k=1 for n=4. For n=5, we can try to use {y=x, x=1, y=1, x+y=5, x+y=6}? But that's 5 lines, with y=x sunny, and the rest are non‑sunny. However, does that cover T_5? Let's test quickly. T_5 points: (1,1)-(1,5), (2,1)-(2,5), (3,1)-(3,3), (4,1)-(4,2), (5,1). The lines:
- x=1 covers column a=1.
- y=1 covers row b=1.
- x+y=5 covers (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1).
- x+y=6 covers (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1).
Now, the points on y=x are (1,1),(2,2),(3,3). Are they covered? (1,1) is covered by x=1 and y=1. (2,2) is not on any of these lines? x=2 not covered, y=2 not covered, x+y=4 not covered. So (2,2) is uncovered. So that fails. So we need a different construction for k=1 for general n.

Let's think of a construction that works for all n. The idea is to use the line y = x (sunny) to cover the diagonal points, and then use the n-1 lines that are all of the form x = a (vertical) for a=1,…, n-1? That would be n-1 lines, covering columns a=1..n-1. The points on column n (a=n) are only (n,1), which would be covered by y=1? But we have n-1 vertical lines, plus y=x, total n lines. But the vertical lines are non‑sunny. Let's test with n=5. Use L1: y=x (sunny). L2: x=1 (non‑sunny). L3: x=2 (non‑sunny). L4: x=3 (non‑sunny). L5: x=4 (non‑sunny). That's 5 lines. Do they cover T_5? The points in column a=1..4 are all covered by the vertical lines. The points with a=5 are (5,1) only. Is (5,1) covered? No, because vertical lines are at a=1..4, and y=x covers (5,5) not (5,1). So (5,1) is uncovered. We could include y=1 as a horizontal line instead of one of the vertical lines. That gives 2 non‑sunny lines (y=1) plus 4 vertical lines? That would be 6 lines, too many. So we need exactly n lines. So we need to choose a set of n-1 non‑sunny lines and 1 sunny line such that all points are covered. The sunny line covers some points. The non‑sunny lines cover the rest.

Perhaps we can use the line y = x (sunny) and then the lines x = 1, x = 2, …, x = n-1 (n-1 lines). As noted, column n's point (n,1) is uncovered. Could we cover (n,1) with the sunny line? No. So we need to modify. Instead, use y = x (sunny) and then the lines y = 1, y = 2, …, y = n-1? That would cover rows b=1..n-1. The point (n,1) is on row b=1, so covered by y=1. The point (n,2) is not in T_n for n≥3? Actually (n,2) has sum n+2 > n+1, so not present. The points with a=n and b=1 only. So covering rows b=1..n-1 would cover (n,1) if we include y=1. But we need n lines total. So we could take:
- sunny: y = x
- non‑sunny: y = 1, y = 2, …, y = n-1? That's n-1 lines, but they are all horizontal. That gives total n lines. Does this cover T_n? The points on these rows are those with b=1..n-1. For each b, the points are (a,b) with a ≤ n+1-b. So the union of rows b=1..n-1 covers all points with b ≤ n-1. The points with b=n are (1,n) only, but (1,n) has sum n+1, which is allowed. Is (1,n) covered? Row y=n is not present. So (1,n) is uncovered. However, (1,n) lies on the line x=1, which is not present. So we need to cover (1,n). Could we include x=1 as a non‑sunny line instead of one of the horizontal lines? But we need exactly n lines. So maybe we can use a combination of vertical and horizontal lines that cover the boundary. For instance, take the three boundary lines: x=1, y=1, x+y=n+1. That's 3 non‑sunny lines. Then we have n-3 lines left. If we want k=1, we need to have exactly 1 sunny line among those n-3 lines. But n-3 lines are many; we could make them all non‑sunny, giving k=0. To get k=1, we need to replace some of them with a sunny line. However, the number of non‑sunny lines is m = n - k. If k=1, then m = n-1 non‑sunny lines. So we need to choose n-1 non‑sunny lines and 1 sunny line. The non‑sunny lines can be any type. The sunny line could be y=x. So we need to select n-1 non‑sunny lines that together with y=x cover T_n. That seems possible.

Let's try to construct for general n. We'll attempt: 
- sunny: L_s = y = x.
- non‑sunny: L_1: x = 1, L_2: y = 1, and also L_3: x + y = n+1? But that's 3 lines, not n-1. So we need many more non‑sunny lines. Perhaps we can take all the lines of the form x = a for a=1,…,n-1 (n-1 lines) and then also include y = x as sunny. That's n lines total. As we saw, that leaves (n,1) uncovered. So we need to cover (n,1). Could we include y=1 as a non‑sunny line instead of one of the vertical lines? For example, take x = 1,…, n-2 (n-2 lines) and also y = 1 (one line), and then y = x (sunny). That's (n-2)+1+1 = n lines. Does this cover (n,1)? y=1 covers (n,1). The points with a=n and b=1 are covered. The points with a=1..n-2 are covered by the vertical lines. The points with a=n-1? Those are (n-1,b) with b up to n. But we have no vertical line at a=n-1. The points on column a=n-1 are (n-1,1),(n-1,2),…,(n-1,n+1-(n-1)=2? Actually a=n-1, b ≤ n+1-(n-1)=2, so (n-1,1),(n-1,2). Are they covered? (n-1,1) is covered by y=1. (n-1,2) is not on y=1 nor on y=x? y=x would give (n-1,n-1) not (n-1,2). So (n-1,2) is uncovered. So we need to cover that. We could add another non‑sunny line like x = n-1. That would increase number of lines. But we already have n lines. So we need to choose n-1 non‑sunny lines and 1 sunny line. That means we have to cover all points with those n-1 non‑sunny lines, except possibly some that are covered by the sunny line. So we need to choose a set of n-1 non‑sunny lines (axis-aligned or diagonal) that together with the sunny line cover T_n. That seems nontrivial but maybe there is a pattern.

Let's step back and think about a general construction for k=1 that works for all n. We can use the following idea: Use the line x = 1 (vertical) to cover all points with a=1. That's n points. Use the line y = 1 (horizontal) to cover all points with b=1. That's n points, overlapping at (1,1). So together they cover 2n-1 points. Then we have n-2 lines left. Those n-2 lines could be taken as the lines x+y = c for c = 3,4,…, n+1? That's n-2 lines? Actually c from 3 to n+1 gives n-1 lines. Let's compute: n+1 - 3 + 1 = n-1. So that's n-1 lines, not n-2. So we need to be careful with counts.

We need a total of n lines. So if we use x=1 and y=1 (2 lines), then we need n-2 more lines. The remaining points after removing column 1 and row 1 are those with a≥2, b≥2, a+b ≤ n+1. That's a triangle of size (n-1) after translation. That triangle can be covered by n-2 lines of slope -1 (diagonals). For example, take the lines x+y = c for c = 4,5,…, n+1? That's (n+1 - 4 + 1) = n-2 lines. Indeed, c from 4 to n+1 gives n-2 lines. But note that we already used x=1 and y=1, which are non‑sunny. The lines x+y = c are also non‑sunny. So overall we have 2 + (n-2) = n lines, all non‑sunny, k=0. That's not good.

To get k=1, we need to replace one of these non‑sunny lines with a sunny line. For instance, we could keep x=1 and y=1, and then for the remaining n-2 lines, we take all the lines x+y = c for c = 4,5,…, n+1 (n-2 lines) except one of them, say c = 4, and replace it with a sunny line. That would give 2 + (n-3) + 1 = n lines, with 1 sunny line. The sunny line could be y = x (slope 1). But does that covering work? We need to check that all points are covered. The points on the line x+y=4 are covered by the sunny line? The sunny line y=x passes through (2,2) which is on x+y=4. But also it passes through (1,1) and (3,3). So it covers some points. However, the points on the line x+y=4 that are not on y=x are (1,3) and (3,1). Are they covered by other lines? They are covered by x=1 or y=1? (1,3) is on x=1, covered. (3,1) is on y=1, covered. So that's fine. The points on x+y=4 are (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). So all are covered. The points on x+y=5 are (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Those are covered by x=1,y=1 or by the line x+y=5 (which we kept). So that works. So the replacement seems plausible.

Now, we need to ensure that the sunny line (y=x) is indeed sunny, and that the other lines are non‑sunny. Also, the set of lines must be distinct. We have x=1, y=1, and the lines x+y = c for c = 4,5,…, n+1 (n-2 lines) but we replaced c=4 with y=x. So we have:
- L1: x=1 (non‑sunny)

- L4: x+y=5 (non‑sunny)
- L5: x+y=6 (non‑sunny)
- …
- L_{n}: x+y = n+1 (non‑sunny)
Wait, we have n lines total. The lines x+y=c for c=5..n+1 give n-1 lines. Adding x=1,y=1 gives 2 + (n-1) = n+1, which is too many. So we need to adjust counts.

Let's compute: For n=5, T_5 points. If we take x=1,y=1, and then we need 3 more lines. The lines x+y=c for c=4..8 (5 lines) would be too many. We need exactly n=5 lines. So we need to choose a subset of lines covering T_n. We can attempt to use x=1 and y=1, and then use the lines x+y=c for c=3,4,...,n+1? That's n-1 lines. Total 2 + (n-1) = n+1. So we cannot include all. So we need to omit some.

But our earlier idea for k=1 was to take x=1,y=1, and then some diagonals, but we need to include a sunny line. So maybe we can take:

Let's think systematically: We want to cover T_n with n lines. Suppose we take the n lines to be:

Now, can we
